import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a transposed convolution, applies GELU, and normalizes with GroupNorm.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, groups, num_groups):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride)
        self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups=num_groups, num_channels=out_channels)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = torch.nn.functional.gelu(x)
        x = self.group_norm(x)
        return x

batch_size   = 128  
in_channels  = 64  
out_channels = 64  
height = width = 256  
kernel_size  = 3
stride       = 1
groups = 8
num_groups = 8

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, groups, num_groups]

We need to fuse all of this into a single CUDA kernel: for each output pixel, we compute conv_transpose value (sum over input channels, kernel), add bias, apply GELU, then apply group norm across the group? Wait group norm requires computing mean and variance per group across spatial dimensions and batch? Actually GroupNorm(num_groups, num_channels) computes mean and variance per group across the channels in the group and across the spatial dimensions (H, W), but NOT across batch? Let's recall: In PyTorch, GroupNorm computes over the mini-batch for each group. The shape is (N, C, H, W). It computes mean and variance for each sample in batch and each group. Specifically, it divides C into num_groups, each group has C/num_channels channels. For each (n, group), it computes mean and variance over (C_group, H, W). So it's NOT across batch; it's per-sample. GroupNorm has affine parameters per channel. So fusing conv + gelu + groupnorm is tricky because groupnorm requires per-sample, per-group statistics that depend on the conv+gelu outputs for the entire sample (all channels in group and all spatial positions). Fusing all into one pass would require a reduction across the entire sample's output. We could tile and use shared memory to compute per-group statistics. Alternatively, we could do a two-pass kernel: first compute conv+gelu output into an intermediate buffer, then compute groupnorm. But that would be memory-bound. However the reference already does three separate kernels (conv, gelu, groupnorm). The custom kernel could still be a two-pass fused approach or a single-pass with shared reductions. For each output pixel, each group has 8 input channels and 8 output channels. So each output channel receives contributions from 8 input channels, kernel 3x3 = 9 weights. That's 72 multiplies per output pixel per output channel. Total output pixels: 128*64*258*258 ≈ 544M. Total MACs: 544M * 8 * 9 = 544M * 72 ≈ 39.2 GMACs. At GPU 10+ TFLOPS, that's maybe 1-2 ms. Could do a single kernel that over a tile computes conv outputs, writes to shared memory, then does warp-level reductions to compute mean/var, then applies normalization and writes final output. But the reduction is per sample and per group across all spatial positions and channels in the group. The output is huge, so each thread block likely handles a subset of spatial positions and channels. To compute mean and variance globally, we might need a two-step reduction or atomic accumulators across the entire sample/group. Another approach: use shared memory per block for partial sums, then block-level reduction (e.g., grid-sync or cooperative groups) or atomic adds to global memory. Since we have many blocks per sample, we can accumulate per-sample per-group partial sums into global memory using atomics. Then within each block, after computing all output values, we reload the final mean and variance (other blocks may not have finished) — needing a synchronization across all blocks for that sample. That's complex. Alternative: compute the conv+gelu into a temporary tensor (maybe in fp16? No, fp32) and then write a highly optimized groupnorm kernel that fuses the affine transform. What if I process the output in tiles and for each tile, load the required input region into shared memory? For output tile (th, tw) at position (h0, w0): - Output positions: (h0 + i, w0 + j) for i in [0, th-1], j in [0, tw-1] - Required input h range: [max(0, h0-2), min(255, h0+th-1)] Actually for each output position h, input needed is [h-2, h] intersect [0, 255]. For the whole tile: [h0-2, h0+th-1] intersect [0, 255]. - Input tile size: roughly (th+2) x (tw+2) spatial positions, but shifted. For th=16, tw=16: input needed is about 18x18 = 324 spatial positions per input channel. For 64 input channels: 324 * 64 floats = 82,944 floats = 331 KB. That's too big for shared memory. We need to process input channels in slices. For example, process 8 input channels at a time: - Input slice: 18x18x8 floats = 2,592 floats = 10.4 KB - Weights: 8 input channels × 64 output channels × 3 × 3 = 4,608 floats = 18.4 KB - Accumulators: 16x16x64 floats = 16,384 floats = 65.5 KB (could be in registers) - Total shared: 10.4 + 18.4 = 28.8 KB. Good! But we accumulate across input channel slices. With 8 slices of 8 input channels: - Accumulators need to persist across slices. If each thread handles one output pixel across all 64 channels, that's 64 floats per thread. - For 256 threads (16x16 tile), that's 16,384 floats total = 64 KB in registers. That's fine. Wait, but GroupNorm needs all 64 channels per position. So each thread must indeed compute all 64 channels. Let me design the kernel: ```cuda // Grid: (batch, h_tiles, w_tiles) // Block: (tile_h, tile_w) threads, e.g., 16x16 = 256 threads // Each thread computes all 64 output channels for one spatial position template<int TILE_H=16, int TILE_W=16> __global__ void fused_convtranspose_gelu_groupnorm( const float* input, // [N, 64, 256, 256] const float* weight, // [64, 64, 3, 3] (ConvTranspose2d weight format) const float* gamma, // [64] const float* beta, // [64] float* output, // [N, 64, 258, 258] int N, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out ) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h0 = blockIdx.y * TILE_H; int w0 = blockIdx.z * TILE_W; int local_h = threadIdx.y; int local_w = threadIdx.x; int h = h0 + local_h; int w = w0 + local_w; if (h >= H_out || w >= W_out) return; float acc[64]; // accumulator for 64 output channels #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) acc[oc] = 0.0f; // Process input channels in slices, loading weights into shared memory // Actually, for a 3x3x64 weight, we can load all weights into shared memory // or keep them in global memory with good caching. // Each thread accesses the same weight for all input channels. for (int ic = 0; ic < 64; ic++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int ih = h - kh; int iw = w - kw; float x_val = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H_in && iw >= 0 && iw < W_in) { x_val = input[((n * 64 + ic) * H_in + ih) * W_in + iw]; } #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) { float w_val = weight[((ic * 64 + oc) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; acc[oc] += x_val * w_val; } } } } // Apply GELU #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) { acc[oc] = gelu(acc[oc]); } // GroupNorm: 8 groups, 8 channels per group #pragma unroll for (int g = 0; g < 8; g++) { float mean = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < 8; c++) { mean += acc[g * 8 + c]; } mean *= 0.125f; // / 8 float var = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < 8; c++) { float diff = acc[g * 8 + c] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var *= 0.125f; // / 8 float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < 8; c++) { int oc = g * 8 + c; acc[oc] = (acc[oc] - mean) * inv_std * gamma[oc] + beta[oc]; } } // Write output #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) { output[((n * 64 + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc[oc]; } } ``` This kernel has each thread computing 64 output channels. But the problem is that the inner loop over `oc` for weights causes each thread to load 64 different weights per (ic, kh, kw), which are contiguous in memory (since weight is [64, 64, 3, 3] and the oc dimension is contiguous? Actually wait - each thread accesses ALL weights. I'll use float4 for weight loads. Wait, actually there's a subtle point about ConvTranspose2d weight layout. In PyTorch: ConvTranspose2d weight is [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_h, kernel_w] For us: [64, 64, 3, 3] When iterating over `oc`, weight[ic, oc, kh, kw] is contiguous in `oc`. So for fixed ic, kh, kw, loading 64 contiguous floats for all oc is efficient. In fact, I can load 16 float4 values to get all 64 weights for a given (ic, kh, kw). Similarly for bias, gamma, beta - all are [64] and contiguous. For the input, for a fixed (ic, h, w), input is [N, C, H, W] = contiguous in W, then H, then C. For our access pattern where h and w vary per thread, and ic varies in outer loop, we access `input[n, ic, h-kh, w-kw]`. For fixed n, ic, kh, kw, each thread accesses a different (h, w), and h-kh varies by thread. This is NOT contiguous across threads unless h-kh is the same, which happens when kh=0 and all threads have the same h... well, each thread has a different h and w, so input accesses are scattered. But if we load the input tile into shared memory cooperatively, this solves the scattered access issue. Let me design the shared memory input loading: For output tile covering [h0, h0+15] × [w0, w0+15]: Required input spatial region: [h0-2, h0+15] × [w0-2, w0+15] Clipped to [0, 255] in both dimensions. Size: up to 18×18. For a slice of 8 input channels: - Shared memory array: `s_input[18*18*8]` - Load cooperatively: 324*8 = 2592 floats. With 256 threads, each loads ~10 floats. Loading pattern: ```cuda int s_input_size = sh * sw * IC_SLICE; for (int idx = tid; idx < s_input_size; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / (sh * sw); int rem = idx % (sh * sw); int local_sh = rem / sw; int local_sw = rem % sw; int ih = h0 - 2 + local_sh; int iw = w0 - 2 + local_sw; int ic = ic_base + c; float val = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H_in && iw >= 0 && iw < W_in && ic < C_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + ic) * H_in + ih) * W_in + iw]; } s_input[idx] = val; } ``` This gives coalesced access since threads with consecutive `tid` load consecutive floats. For weights, into shared memory: ```cuda int s_weight_size = IC_SLICE * C_out * 3 * 3; // 8 * 64 * 9 = 4608 for (int idx = tid; idx < s_weight_size; idx += block_size) { // weight layout: [in_channels, out_channels, 3, 3] // We want to load ic in [ic_base, ic_base+7], all oc, all kh, kw // idx maps to: ic_offset, oc, kh, kw // where idx = ((ic_offset * C_out + oc) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw int kw = idx % 3; int tmp = idx / 3; int kh = tmp % 3; tmp = tmp / 3; int oc = tmp % C_out; int ic_offset = tmp / C_out; int ic = ic_base + ic_offset; s_weight[idx] = weight[((ic * C_out + oc) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; } ``` Again, coalesced. Then computation: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int ih = threadIdx.y - kh + 2; // because h0 maps to sh=2 in shared mem int iw = threadIdx.x - kw + 2; // Actually, need to map properly. // In shared mem, spatial position (ih, iw) corresponds to global input at (h0-2+ih, w0-2+iw) // For output at (h0+th, w0+tw), we need input at (h0+th-kh, w0+tw-kw) // which is at shared mem spatial indices: (th-kh+2, tw-kw+2) // But only if these are in [0, sh-1] and [0, sw-1] int s_ih = threadIdx.y + 2 - kh; int s_iw = threadIdx.x + 2 - kw; if (s_ih >= 0 && s_ih < sh && s_iw >= 0 && s_iw < sw) { int input_idx = ((c) * sh + s_ih) * sw + s_iw; float x_val = s_input[input_idx]; // Load 64 weights for this (c, kh, kw) // s_weight layout: [ic_offset][oc][kh][kw] #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) { int w_idx = ((c * 64 + oc) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw; acc[oc] += x_val * s_weight[w_idx]; } } } } ``` Wait, this has `c` in the loop, which is the slice index. The outer loop is over ic_slices: ```cuda for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < 64; ic_base += 8) { // load input and weights // then for c from 0 to 7: for (int c = 0; c < 8; c++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { // use s_input and s_weight } } } } ``` Actually, let's write it more cleanly: ```cuda for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < C_in; ic_base += IC_SLICE) { __syncthreads(); // load s_input and s_weight cooperatively __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < IC_SLICE; c++) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int s_ih = threadIdx.y + 2 - kh; int s_iw = threadIdx.x + 2 - kw; if (s_ih >= 0 && s_ih < sh && s_iw >= 0 && s_iw < sw) { float x_val = s_input[(c * sh + s_ih) * sw + s_iw]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) { float w_val = s_weight[((c * 64 + oc) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; acc[oc] += x_val * w_val; } } } } } } ``` This looks reasonable. The `if` inside the kh, kw loop can be optimized by unrolling and specializing for the cases where the condition is always true (interior of tile). But with 16x16 tile and kh, kw in [0,2], only the threads near edges have the `if` false for some (kh, kw). Most threads have all combinations valid. Actually, for s_ih = threadIdx.y + 2 - kh: threadIdx.y ranges from 0 to 15. kh ranges from 0 to 2. s_ih ranges from 0 - 0 + 2 = 2 to 15 - 2 + 2 = 15. Wait, let me recalculate: s_ih = threadIdx.y + 2 - kh For kh=0: s_ih = threadIdx.y + 2, range [2, 17] For kh=1: s_ih = threadIdx.y + 1, range [1, 16] For kh=2: s_ih = threadIdx.y + 0, range [0, 15] And sh = TILE_H + 2 = 18. So s_ih is always in [0, 17]. For sh=18: - kh=0: s_ih in [2, 17], always < 18. OK. - kh=1: s_ih in [1, 16], always < 18. OK. - kh=2: s_ih in [0, 15], always >= 0 and < 18. OK. Similarly for s_iw = threadIdx.x + 2 - kw with sw=18: - kw=0: s_iw in [2, 17], always < 18 - kw=1: s_iw in [1, 16], always < 18 - kw=2: s_iw in [0, 15], always >= 0 and < 18 So for the interior of the output (not near global boundaries), the `if` is ALWAYS true. We only need the boundary check for the global output bounds (h >= H_out or w >= W_out), and for the input bounds when the tile is near image edges. Wait, but the shared memory input tile is padded with zeros for out-of-bounds input regions. So the `if` for s_ih/s_iw is always true because shared memory covers exactly the needed region. The global input bounds are already handled during loading (out-of-bounds regions are loaded as 0). So the computation loop has NO conditionals! That's great. However, we DO need to skip threads that are outside the global output bounds. We handle this at the beginning: ```cuda if (h >= H_out || w >= W_out) { // either return early, or participate in shared memory loading but don't compute/write } ``` If we return early, the thread doesn't participate in __syncthreads(). That's a deadlock. So we must NOT return early. Instead, threads outside bounds should participate in shared memory loading but skip computation and writing. Actually, a simpler approach: launch enough blocks to cover the output, and have the block dimensions match the tile. The last blocks in y and z dimensions might be partially outside. For those, threads outside bounds can just not compute/write. Let me redesign with a "guard" variable: ```cuda bool valid = (h < H_out && w < W_out); ``` And skip computation and writing if !valid. But participate in __syncthreads(). This means all threads in the block load shared memory. For the computation, valid threads do the loops. Non-valid threads can skip to save a tiny bit of compute, but it's negligible. OK, I think this is a solid plan. Let me write the kernel. But I want to consider one more optimization: using half2 or float2/float4 for weight and accumulator access. Since the weight access for different oc is contiguous, we can use float4. ```cuda for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc += 4) { float4 w4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(&s_weight[...])[oc / 4]; acc[oc] += x_val * w4.x; acc[oc+1] += x_val * w4.y; acc[oc+2] += x_val * w4.z; acc[oc+3] += x_val * w4.w; } ``` This should improve throughput. Actually, for the accumulator loop, we can structure it as: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc += 4) { float4 w4 = *reinterpret_cast<float4*>(&s_weight[w_base + oc]); acc[oc+0] += x_val * w4.x; acc[oc+1] += x_val * w4.y; acc[oc+2] += x_val * w4.z; acc[oc+3] += x_val * w4.w; } ``` Wait, only if `s_weight[w_base + oc]` is 16-byte aligned. Since `w_base = ((c * 64 + 0) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw`, and c, kh, kw might not make this 16-byte aligned relative to the start of s_weight if 64 isn't a multiple of... well, 64 is a multiple of 4, so `((c * 64 + oc) * 9 + kh * 3 + kw)` for oc=0: the address is `&s_weight[c * 64 * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]`. Is this 16-byte aligned? c * 64 * 9 = c * 576. For c=0, offset is kh*3+kw, which ranges from 0 to 8. Only when kh*3+kw is a multiple of 4 is it aligned. So alignment is NOT guaranteed for all (kh, kw). This is tricky. We could pad each (c, kh, kw)'s weight slice to 16 bytes, or we could just ensure alignment by padding s_weight layout. But that's getting complicated. Actually, a simpler approach: use half2 or just rely on the compiler to vectorize. Modern CUDA compilers are pretty good at vectorizing loads from shared memory when they see contiguous access patterns. Alternatively, we can restructure s_weight as: `s_weight[IC_SLICE][3][3][64]` where each [64] slice is 256 bytes and naturally aligned if we pad appropriately. But in CUDA, arrays of float[64] are aligned to 4 bytes, not 16 bytes. To guarantee 16-byte alignment, we could use `float4` explicitly. Let me think... Actually, for shared memory, alignment is relative to the start of the shared memory array. If we declare: `__shared__ float s_weight[IC_SLICE * C_out * 9];` The start is aligned. We just need each 64-element oc-slice to start at a 16-byte boundary. Since 64 is a multiple of 4, and the offset for each (c, kh, kw) is `c*64*9 + kh*3*64 + kw*64 = 64*(c*9 + kh*3 + kw)`. Since 64 is divisible by 4, every oc-slice starts at a 16-byte boundary! YES! `64 * anything` is always divisible by 64 (bytes), which is certainly divisible by 16! So `s_weight[((c * 64 + oc) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]` - the base address for oc=0 is: `&s_weight[c * 64 * 9 + kh * 3 * 64 + kw * 64]` Offset in floats: `64 * (c * 9 + kh * 3 + kw)` Offset in bytes: `256 * (c * 9 + kh * 3 + kw)` This is always divisible by 256, so definitely 16-byte aligned! Great, we can use float4 loads for the weights. For the input, since x_val is the same for all oc in the inner loop, it's already optimal - loaded once, broadcast to all. For accumulators, we can use float4 to update them too: ```cuda float4 a4 = *reinterpret_cast<float4*>(&acc[oc]); a4.x += x_val * w4.x; a4.y += x_val * w4.y; a4.z += x_val * w4.z; a4.w += x_val * w4.w; *reinterpret_cast<float4*>(&acc[oc]) = a4; ``` This should be efficient. Actually, for the accumulators, `acc` is in registers, so alignment is guaranteed if we declare it as `float acc[64]`. The compiler should handle vectorized access. Let me write the complete kernel. I also need to handle bias addition before GELU. GELU formula: `0.5*x*(1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi)*(x + 0.044715*x^3)))` Or the approximate form. PyTorch uses this exact formulation. I'll use: ```cuda inline float gelu(float x) { float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608028654f * (x + 0.044714998453855515f * x * x * x))); return x * cdf; } ``` Actually, PyTorch's GELU implementation uses the exact tanh approximation. Let me check: gelu(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) Yes. For GroupNorm with eps: PyTorch uses 1e-5 by default. So: ```cuda float mean = ...; float var = ...; for (int c = 0; c < 8; c++) { int oc = g * 8 + c; float normalized = (acc[oc] - mean) * rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); acc[oc] = normalized * gamma[oc] + beta[oc]; } ``` Now, one issue: I should make sure the kernel handles the exact dimensions. H_out and W_out need to be computed from H_in, W_in, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding. For ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, kernel=3, padding=0, dilation=1, output_padding=0: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 = H_in - 1 + 2 + 1 = H_in + 2 = 258 So H_out = H_in + 2, W_out = W_in + 2. In the constructor, we can compute these values and pass them to the kernel. Actually, I should compute them in `ModelNew.__init__` and pass them in. Now, let me finalize the kernel design and write it. Actually, let me also consider using `__launch_bounds__` to help the compiler manage registers. With 256 threads and wanting ~64 registers, we could specify `__launch_bounds__(256, 3)` to hint at 3 blocks per SM, which is 256*3=768 threads / 65536 registers ≈ 85 registers per thread. That might help. OK, let me write the Python file with the kernel. Wait, I realize I should first check if the reference uses `bias=True` by default. PyTorch ConvTranspose2d defaults to `bias=True`. But maybe in this reference, since they don't explicitly set bias, it defaults to True. Yes. So our kernel needs to add bias. However, let me check one more thing. In the reference constructor: ```python self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride) ``` Default values in nn.ConvTranspose2d: - bias: True - padding: 0 - output_padding: 0 - groups: 1 - dilation: 1 So yes, there is bias. Let me write the complete code now. Actually, I want to make sure I understand the exact layout of ConvTranspose2d weights in PyTorch. Weight shape is [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_h, kernel_w]. Let me double check by looking at PyTorch source or docs. Yes, ConvTranspose2d weight is (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size[0], kernel_size[1]). Great. Now, the model init extracts parameters. In ModelNew, we need to extract weight and bias from the conv_transpose module, and gamma (weight) and beta (bias) from the group_norm module. One concern: `nn.GroupNorm` has `weight` and `bias` attributes, which are [num_channels]. The formula is: y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias So our kernel needs to apply this. Also, note that in the reference, the conv output goes through GELU, then GroupNorm. That means the GroupNorm operates on the GELU-activated values. Now, let me write the complete ModelNew class. I also realize that compiling a kernel with 64 unrolled accumulators and float4 accesses might take a while. The compile time might be high, but we only compile once. Oh, and one more thing: for the shared memory, we need to be careful about bank conflicts. Accessing s_weight for all oc with float4 should be conflict-free since each thread reads its own float4s (but wait, all threads read the SAME weights). Actually, in our design, ALL threads in the block read the same weights from shared memory! This is a broadcast. In shared memory, broadcast reads are handled efficiently - no bank conflicts for broadcast. And all threads read different input values from s_input (for fixed c, kh, kw, each thread reads the input at its own spatial position). For s_input layout `s_input[c][sh][sw]`, and threads accessing position (threadIdx.y, threadIdx.x), if sw causes bank conflicts... Let me think. Shared memory banks are 4 bytes. `s_input[(c * sh + s_ih) * sw + s_iw]`. For fixed c, s_ih, if threads with consecutive threadIdx.x access consecutive s_iw, and if sw is odd or not a multiple of 32, there might be bank conflicts. sw = TILE_W + 2. For TILE_W=16, sw=18. Threads (threadIdx.y=0, threadIdx.x=0..15) access s_input[s_ih * 18 + s_iw]. s_iw = threadIdx.x + 2 - kw. For fixed kw, consecutive threads access consecutive memory addresses. Consecutive addresses map to consecutive banks. Since there are 32 banks and sw=18, after 16 threads, the bank index wraps around. With 32 banks, 16 consecutive threads access 16 different banks (0 to 15), no conflicts. Great! What if we use TILE_W=32? sw=34. 32 consecutive threads access banks 0..31, no conflicts. 2 + s_ih;\n int iw = w0 - 2 + s_iw;\n int ic = ic_base + c;\n \n float val = 0.0f;\n if (n < N && ic < C_in && ih >= 0 && ih < H_in && iw >= 0 && iw < W_in) {\n val = input[((n * C_in + ic) * H_in + ih) * W_in + iw];\n }\n s_input[idx] = val;\n }\n \n // Load weights into shared memory\n int s_weight_size = IC_SLICE * C_out * 9;\n for (int idx = tid; idx < s_weight_size; idx += block_size) {\n int kw = idx % 3;\n int t = idx / 3;\n int kh = t % 3;\n t = t / 3;\n int oc = t % C_out;\n int c = t / C_out;\n int ic = ic_base + c;\n \n float val = 0.0f;\n if (ic < C_in) {\n val = weight[((ic * C_out + oc) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw];\n }\n s_weight[idx] = val;\n }\n \n __syncthreads();\n \n if (valid) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int c = 0; c < IC_SLICE; c++) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) {\n int s_ih = local_h + 2 - kh;\n int s_iw = local_w + 2 - kw;\n float x_val = s_input[(c * sh + s_ih) * sw + s_iw];\n \n int w_base = ((c * C_out + 0) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc += 4) {\n float4 w4 = *reinterpret_cast<float4*>(&s_weight[w_base + oc]);\n acc[oc + 0] += x_val * w4.x;\n acc[oc + 1] += x_val * w4.y;\n acc[oc + 2] += x_val * w4.z;\n acc[oc + 3] += x_val * w4.w;\n }\n }\n }\n }\n }\n }\n \n if (valid) {\n // Add bias\n #pragma unroll\n for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) {\n acc[oc] += bias[oc];\n }\n \n // GELU\n #pragma unroll\n for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) {\n acc[oc] = gelu_approx(acc[oc]);\n }\n \n // GroupNorm\n int channels_per_group = C_out / num_groups; // 64 / 8 = 8\n #pragma unroll\n for (int g = 0; g < num_groups; g++) {\n float mean = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int c = 0; c < channels_per_group; c++) {\n mean += acc[g * channels_per_group + c];\n }\n mean *= (1.0f / channels_per_group);\n \n float var = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int c = 0; c < channels_per_group; c++) {\n float diff = acc[g * channels_per_group + c] - mean;\n var += diff * diff;\n }\n var *= (1.0f / channels_per_group);\n \n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int c = 0; c < channels_per_group; c++) {\n int oc = g * channels_per_group + c;\n acc[oc] = (acc[oc] - mean) * inv_std * gamma[oc] + beta[oc];\n }\n }\n \n // Write output\n #pragma unroll\n for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; oc++) {\n output[((n * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc[oc];\n }\n }\n}\n\nvoid fused_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor weight,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n torch::Tensor gamma,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n torch::Tensor output,\n int N, int C_in, int H_in, int W_in,\n int C_out, int H_out, int W_out,\n int num_groups\n) {\n dim3 block(TILE_W, TILE_H);\n dim3 grid(N, (H_out + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H, (W_out + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W);\n \n fused_convtranspose_gelu_groupnorm_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n weight.data_ptr<float>(),\n bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n gamma.data_ptr<float>(),\n beta.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C_in, H_in, W_in,\n C_out, H_out, W_out,\n num_groups\n );\n}\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='fused_convtranspose_gelu_groupnorm',\n cpp_sources='''\n #include <torch/extension.h>\n void fused_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor weight,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n torch::Tensor gamma,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n torch::Tensor output,\n int N, int C_in, int H_in, int W_in,\n int C_out, int H_out, int W_out,\n int num_groups\n );\n ''',\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['fused_forward'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, groups, num_groups):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride)\n self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups=num_groups, num_channels=out_channels)\n \n self.N = None\n self.C_in = in_channels\n self.C_out = out_channels\n self.H_in = None\n self.W_in = None\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.stride = stride\n self.num_groups = num_groups\n \n def forward(self, x):\n N, C, H, W = x.shape\n if self.N != N or self.H_in != H or self.W_in != W:\n self.N = N\n self.H_in = H\n self.W_in = W\n # Compute output dimensions for ConvTranspose2d with padding=0, output_padding=0\n self.H_out = (H - 1) * self.stride - 2 * 0 + 1 * (self.kernel_size - 1) + 0 + 1\n self.W_out = (W - 1) * self.stride - 2 * 0 + 1 * (self.kernel_size - 1) + 0 + 1\n \n output = torch.empty(N, self.C_out, self.H_out, self.W_out, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)\n \n module.fused_forward(\n x,\n self.conv_transpose.weight,\n self.conv_transpose.bias,\n self.group_norm.weight,\n self.group_norm.bias,\n output,\n N, self.C_in, self.H_in, self.W_in,\n self.C_out, self.H_out, self.W_out,\n self.num_groups\n )\n \n return output\n" }

I need to fix the shared memory issues. The shared memory arrays can't have variable length that's not compile-time constant in CUDA. Let me use constant sizes or dynamic shared memory. I'll use compile-time constants for the tile sizes and just allocate fixed-size arrays in shared memory. Let me rethink the tiling strategy. Since the parameters are fixed for this problem: - N = 128, C_in = 64, H_in = 256, W_in = 256 - C_out = 64, H_out = 256, W_out = 256 - kernel_size = 3, stride = 1 - groups = 8, num_groups = 8 For the shared memory arrays, I'll use maximum sizes or just fixed sizes large enough for the cases. Alternatively, I can use `extern __shared__ float s_mem[];` for dynamic shared memory. Let me simplify by using fixed compile-time constants for everything and use dynamic shared memory for flexibility. Actually, the simplest fix is to use compile-time constants. Given the fixed batch size, I can design a kernel that works well for this specific size. Let me use: - TILE = 16x16 - IC_SLICE = 32 - sh = TILE_H + 2 = 18 (for kernel_size=3) - sw = TILE_W + 2 = 18 But actually, for stride=1, the transposed convolution is just a regular convolution with flipped weights. So the receptive field is still 3x3. Let me use: - sh = 16 + 2 = 18 - sw = 16 + 2 = 18 For shared memory: 32 * 18 * 18 + 32 * 64 * 9 = 10368 + 18432 = 28800 floats = 115200 bytes per block That's well within limits. But wait, the error is because `sh` and `sw` variables are computed at runtime. I need to use fixed compile-time sizes or dynamic shared memory. Let me use dynamic shared memory with `extern __shared__ float s_mem[];`. Also, another issue - the weight layout in shared memory. The original code loads weights with a stride that depends on C_out, and then tries to access them with `float4`. This requires C_out to be a multiple of 4, which it is (64). But the array indexing `w_base + oc` needs to be carefully checked. Let me completely rewrite this kernel more carefully. Actually, let me reconsider the algorithm. Since we're on H100 with 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth, and our problem involves: 1. Input: 128 * 64 * 256 * 256 * 4 bytes = 2 GB 2. Output: 128 * 64 * 256 * 256 * 4 bytes = 2 GB 3. Weights: 64 * 64 * 3 * 3 * 4 = ~147 KB 4. Total memory for 3 separate ops: ~6 GB read + 4 GB write = ~10 GB Even fully fused, if each element is read/written once: that's pretty much bandwidth-bound. The main benefit of fusion is reducing redundant reads/writes of intermediate activations. For a more careful implementation, let me use CUTLASS or a simpler tile-based approach. Actually, let me first think about what the PyTorch reference does: 1. ConvTranspose2d: reads input (2GB) and weights, writes intermediate (2GB) 2. GELU: reads intermediate (2GB), writes (2GB) 3. GroupNorm: reads (2GB), writes (2GB) Total: reads ~8GB, writes ~6GB Fully fused: reads input once + weights, writes output once Total: read ~2GB + weights, write ~2GB That's a 3-4x reduction in memory traffic, which should give significant speedup even with a naive kernel. Let me reimplement with: - Dynamic shared memory - Proper thread coarsening (each thread computes more than 1 output pixel) - Use of float4 where possible Actually, let me start simpler to ensure correctness, then optimize. Given 64 output channels and 64 input channels, let me have each block compute a tile of output and handle all output channels. I'll use a 3D grid: (n, tile_h, tile_w). For better occupancy, I should use smaller blocks. Let me try: - Each block: 16x8 threads (128 threads) - Each thread computes a 1x1 output - Block computes 16x8 spatial tile - All 64 channels Shared memory for input: IC_SLICE * (TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2) With IC_SLICE = 16: 16 * 18 * 10 = 2880 floats Shared memory for weight: IC_SLICE * C_out * 9 = 16 * 64 * 9 = 9216 floats Total: ~48 KB per block That's good for occupancy. But I need to process C_in = 64 in IC_SLICE = 16 chunks = 4 outer iterations. Let me write a cleaner version.

reasoning
The issue is likely in either the conv transpose computation or the GELU approximation. Let me trace through the differences more carefully. For conv_transpose with stride=1, kernel_size=3, padding=0, output_padding=0: - PyTorch: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding = (H - 1) * 1 + 3 = H + 2 Wait, actually my formula for H_out is wrong: For PyTorch ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, kernel_size=3, padding=0, dilation=1, output_padding=0: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size = (H - 1) * 1 + 3 = H + 2 So for H=256, H_out = 258. And for the reverse (computing which input positions contribute to output position (h,w)): input at (h_in, w_in) contributes to output at (h_in, w_in) * stride + kh for each kernel position kh. So output[h, w] = sum_{kh, kw} input[h - kh, w - kw] * weight[kh, kw] For output position (h, w), the contributing input positions are (h-kh, w-kw) for kh in [0, 2] and kw in [0, 2]. But the input is zero-padded with k_l-1 = 2 rows/cols of zeros minimally? Actually, no - the default behavior for conv_transpose (aka deconvolution) is: Output[h, w] = sum_{kh=0}^{K-1} sum_{kw=0}^{K-1} input[h - kh, w - kw] * weight[kh, kw] where input[pos] = 0 if pos is outside [0, H_in-1]. There's no explicit padding. The shapes work out naturally. Let me reconsider. For Conv2d (not transpose), if we had a (258, 258) input and a 3x3 kernel with stride=1, padding=0, the output would be 256x256. The weight arrangement is [C_out, C_in, K, K]. The convolution is: out[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh} sum_{kw} in[n, c_in, h+kh, w+kw] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] For conv_transpose, it's roughly the "transpose": out[n, c_in, h, w] = sum_{c_out} sum_{kh} sum_{kw} in[n, c_out, h+kh, w+kw] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] Wait, let me just verify: for conv_transpose, the weight is stored as [C_in, C_out, K, K], and the output channel dimension is C_out. out[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh=0}^{K-1} sum_{kw=0}^{K-1} in[n, c_in, h+kh, w+kw] * weight[c_in, c_out, K-1-kh, K-1-kw] Actually, the standard formulation for transposed convolution / fractionally strided convolution is: For each input position (c_in, y, x), we place the flipped kernel (relative to standard convolution), scaled by input[y, x], at output position (y*stride, x*stride). For stride=1, padding=0: out[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw: valid} in[n, c_in, h-kh+K-1, w-kw+K-1] * weight[c_in, c_out, K-1-kh, K-1-kw] With K=3: out[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh=0}^{2} sum_{kw=0}^{2} in[n, c_in, h-kh+2, w-kw+2] * weight[c_in, c_out, 2-kh, 2-kw] Let i_h = h - kh + 2, i_w = w - kw + 2. This can also be written with reversed indices. Actually the simplest way: in PyTorch, conv_transpose2d weight is [in_channels, out_channels, K, K] and the output is: out[h,w] = sum_{kh,kw} input[h - kh, w - kw] * weight[kh, kw] BUT with the understanding that this is after flipping the kernel. PyTorch's conv_transpose2d with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to a full convolution (valid correlation with flipped kernel). The exact formula used by PyTorch: For output position h, it sums over kernel positions. If stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0: out[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh=0}^{K-1} sum_{kw=0}^{K-1} input[n, c_in, h + kh, w + kw] * weight[c_in, c_out, K-1-kh, K-1-kw] Let me verify this. If this is correct, then for my kernel: output[h, w] += input[h + kh, w + kw] * weight[2-kh, 2-kw] or equivalently, with variables jh = 2-kh, jw = 2-kw: output[h, w] += input[h + 2 - jh, w + 2 - jw] * weight[jh, jw] So my current code, which computes: x_val = input[h - kh + pad, w - kw + pad] For stride=1 conv transpose, it should be: For h_out = 0 to H_out-1, the input positions that contribute are ... Maybe let me just use PyTorch to test what the exact formula should be with a minimal example.

For convtranspose2d, weight shape is also (1,1,3,3) (since in_channels=1, out_channels=1, groups=1). If conv2d uses cross-correlation: out[h,w] = sum_{kh,kw} in[h+kh, w+kw] * weight[kh,kw] (for padding=0). Then transpose conv computes the adjoint: for output position (i,j), the input positions that map to it are those h,w such that h+kh=i, w+kw=j for some kh,kw in [0,2]. That means h in [i-2,i], w in [j-2,j]. So: out[i,j] = sum_{kh=0}^{2} sum_{kw=0}^{2} in[i-kh, j-kw] * weight[kh,kw] (with valid indices). This is exactly the formula `out[i,j] = sum_{kh,kw} in[i - kh, j - kw] * weight[kh, kw]`, with in clipped to valid range. So for padding=0, the needed input for output i is i, i-1, i-2. Actually, there is a way to get cuDNN's conv output and then apply our kernel without extra overhead: that's what we planned. Let's write the code. Custom kernel `fused_gelu_groupnorm` in CUDA: Input: tensor `x` of shape (N, C, H, W) as NCHW. Output: tensor `y` of shape (N, C, H, W). Parameters: gamma (C), beta (C), num_groups, eps. Grid: (N, num_groups, 1) Block: 256 threads. Inside kernel: - n = blockIdx.x - g = blockIdx.y - C_per_group = C / num_groups - c_start = g * C_per_group - c_end = c_start + C_per_group - tid = threadIdx.x - H, W Shared memory for block reduction: - float s_sum[256]; - float s_sq[256]; (or combine and do two reductions) Algorithm: 1. First pass: compute local sum and sum_sq. float local_sum = 0.0f, local_sq = 0.0f; for (int p = tid; p < H * W; p += blockDim.x) { int h = p / W; int w = p % W; int base = ((n * C + c_start) * H + h) * W + w; // The channels for this group are contiguous? c_start, c_start+1,... c_start+7. // For each c: #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_per_group; ++c) { float v = x[base + c * H * W]; float gval = 0.5f * v * (1.0f + erff(v * 0.7071067811865475f)); local_sum += gval; local_sq += gval * gval; } } 2. Reduce local_sum and local_sq across block. Use warp shuffle to reduce within warp, then store warp sums to shared, then reduce in thread 0. Or use shared mem tree reduction. Since blockDim=256 = 8 warps of 32. Let's use __shfl_down_sync. __device__ inline float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); return val; } Then: local_sum = warp_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sq = warp_reduce_sum(local_sq); if ((tid & 31) == 0) { s_sum[tid / 32] = local_sum; s_sq[tid / 32] = local_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { local_sum = (tid < 8) ? s_sum[tid] : 0.0f; local_sq = (tid < 8) ? s_sq[tid] : 0.0f; local_sum = warp_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sq = warp_reduce_sum(local_sq); if (tid == 0) { s_sum[0] = local_sum; s_sq[0] = local_sq; } } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_sum[0] / (C_per_group * H * W); float var = s_sq[0] / (C_per_group * H * W) - mean * mean; // or compute directly float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); 3. Second pass: write normalized output. for (int p = tid; p < H * W; p += blockDim.x) { int h = p / W; int w = p % W; int base_in = ((n * C + c_start) * H + h) * W + w; int base_out = ((n * C + c_start) * H + h) * W + w; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_per_group; ++c) { int idx = base_in + c * H * W; float v = x[idx]; float gval = 0.5f * v * (1.0f + erff(v * 0.7071067811865475f)); float out_val = (gval - mean) * inv_std * gamma[c_start + c] + beta[c_start + c]; output[idx] = out_val; } } Wait, is the division for `h = p / W` and `w = p % W` expensive for 66564 iterations? 66564 / 256 = 260 iterations per thread. 260 int divs is okay. But the memory access pattern: `base = ((n * C + c_start) * H + h) * W + w`. Then for each c, `idx = base + c * H * W`. Since c is in [0,7], and H*W is 66564, the offsets are large. Are the 8 loads per thread coalesced? For a fixed p and tid, threads in a warp have the same h and w (if they have the same p). But consecutive tid have consecutive p, hence consecutive w (and h when w wraps). So the base address for a given c differs by `delta_p` between threads, which is 1 (if p increments by 1). Wait, `p` increments by blockDim.x (256) between loop iterations, but within one iteration, thread `tid` uses `p = tid + k*256`. So base difference between adjacent threads is 1. So for c=0, addresses are consecutive (coalesced). For c=1, addresses are also consecutive (offset by H*W from c=0). So all loads are coalesced. Great. However, we have an issue: we are doing two passes over the data. In the second pass, we re-read x and recompute GELU. Could we store the GELU'd values in shared memory to avoid recomputation? The total values per block for one iteration of p is 256 pixels * 8 channels = 2048 floats = 8KB. That's small! Actually, in each loop iteration, we process 256 pixels. For those 256 pixels, we load 8 channels each = 2048 values. We could store them in shared memory. But the loop iterates 260 times. We can process in chunks that fit in shared memory. However, we need to compute mean and var across ALL pixels before writing output. So we could do: - First pass: iterate over all pixels. For each chunk of 256 pixels, load x, compute GELU, store GELU values in shared memory, accumulate sum and sum_sq from shared memory. Then discard shared memory and move to next chunk. - After first pass, we have mean and inv_std. - Second pass: iterate over all pixels again, same as before. Code skeleton: ``` __global__ void gelu_groupnorm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups ) { int n = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int c0 = g * (C / num_groups); int Cg = C / num_groups; int tid = threadIdx.x; float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sq = 0.0f; int plane_stride = C * H * W; int group_offset = n * plane_stride + c0 * H * W; // First pass: accumulate for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { int row_offset = group_offset + h * W; for (int w = tid; w < W; w += blockDim.x) { int base = row_offset + w; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < Cg; ++c) { float v = input[base + c * H * W]; float gval = 0.5f * v * (1.0f + erff(v * 0.7071067811865475f)); local_sum += gval; local_sq += gval * gval; } } } float mean = block_reduce_sum(local_sum) / (float)(Cg * H * W); float sq_mean = block_reduce_sum(local_sq) / (float)(Cg * H * W); float var = sq_mean - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); __shared__ float s_mean; __shared__ float s_inv_std; if (tid == 0) { s_mean = mean; s_inv_std = inv_std; } __syncthreads(); // Second pass: write normalized output for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { int row_offset = group_offset + h * W; for (int w = tid; w < W; w += blockDim.x) { int base = row_offset + w; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < Cg; ++c) { int idx = base + c * H * W; float v = input[idx]; float gval = 0.5f * v * (1.0f + erff(v * 0.7071067811865475f)); float out_val = (gval - s_mean) * s_inv_std * gamma[c0 + c] + beta[c0 + c]; output[idx] = out_val; } } } } ``` Wait, `block_reduce_sum` needs to be defined. offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__device__ inline float block_reduce_sum(float val) {\n static __shared__ float shared[32];\n int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;\n int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5;\n val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val;\n __syncthreads();\n val = (threadIdx.x < 32) ? shared[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f;\n if (wid == 0) val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n return val;\n}\n\n__global__ void gelu_groupnorm_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ gamma,\n const float* __restrict__ beta,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups\n) {\n int n = blockIdx.x;\n int g = blockIdx.y;\n int Cg = C / num_groups;\n int c0 = g * Cg;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n \n int group_offset = n * C * H * W + c0 * H * W;\n float local_sum = 0.0f;\n float local_sq = 0.0f;\n \n // First pass: compute GELU and accumulate mean/var statistics\n for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) {\n int row_offset = group_offset + h * W;\n for (int w = tid; w < W; w += BLOCK_SIZE) {\n int base = row_offset + w;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int c = 0; c < Cg; ++c) {\n float v = input[base + c * H * W];\n float gval = gelu_exact(v);\n local_sum += gval;\n local_sq += gval * gval;\n }\n }\n }\n \n float total = (float)(Cg * H * W);\n float mean = block_reduce_sum(local_sum) / total;\n float sq_mean = block_reduce_sum(local_sq) / total;\n float var = sq_mean - mean * mean;\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);\n \n __shared__ float s_mean;\n __shared__ float s_inv_std;\n if (tid == 0) {\n s_mean = mean;\n s_inv_std = inv_std;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // Second pass: apply normalization and write output\n for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) {\n int row_offset = group_offset + h * W;\n for (int w = tid; w < W; w += BLOCK_SIZE) {\n int base = row_offset + w;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int c = 0; c < Cg; ++c) {\n int idx = base + c * H * W;\n float v = input[idx];\n float gval = gelu_exact(v);\n float out_val = (gval - s_mean) * s_inv_std * gamma[c0 + c] + beta[c0 + c];\n output[idx] = out_val;\n }\n }\n }\n}\n\nvoid gelu_groupnorm(\n at::Tensor input,\n at::Tensor gamma,\n at::Tensor beta,\n at::Tensor output,\n int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups\n) {\n dim3 grid(N, num_groups);\n dim3 block(BLOCK_SIZE);\n gelu_groupnorm_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n gamma.data_ptr<float>(),\n beta.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C, H, W, num_groups\n );\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\nvoid gelu_groupnorm(\n at::Tensor input,\n at::Tensor gamma,\n at::Tensor beta,\n at::Tensor output,\n int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups\n);\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='gelu_groupnorm_fused',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['gelu_groupnorm'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, groups, num_groups):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride)\n self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups=num_groups, num_channels=out_channels)\n self.num_groups = num_groups\n \n def forward(self, x):\n x = self.conv_transpose(x)\n N, C, H, W = x.shape\n out = torch.empty_like(x)\n module.gelu_groupnorm(\n x,\n self.group_norm.weight,\n self.group_norm.bias,\n out,\n N, C, H, W,\n self.num_groups\n )\n return out\n" }

Let me think about it this way: the standard conv computes: `y = W * x` (with appropriate padding) The transpose conv computes: `y = W^T * x` For the 2D case with stride=1, the transpose conv computes a "full" convolution. The formula is: For each output (h, w): `output[h, w] = Σ_i,j W[i, j] * input[h - i + padding, w - j + padding]` For transpose conv with kernel=3, stride=1, padding=0: output[h, w] = Σ_kh Σ_kw weight[kh, kw] * input[h - kh + 1, w - kw + 1] where input indices outside [0, H-1] or [0, W-1] are treated as 0. From PyTorch docs: ``` torch.nn.gelu(input, approximate='none') ``` So the exact formula is: `gelu_exact(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` And the approximate formula using tanh is: `gelu_approx(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(√(2/π) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3)))` If the reference uses exact GELU (which is the PyTorch default), then my tanh approximation would introduce differences! Let me verify by looking at the reference again: ```python x = torch.nn.functional.gelu(x) ``` In PyTorch, `F.gelu(x)` without specifying `approximate` defaults to `'none'` (exact). The max difference can be up to a few units for large values. And since we apply GroupNorm afterwards, even small errors can propagate. So I need to either: 1. Use exact GELU in my kernel 2. Or match the approximation used by the reference For exact GELU, I'll use: ```cpp inline __device__ float gelu_exact(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.7071067811865475f)); } ``` Wait but `erff` might be expensive. Let me also double check the GroupNorm computation: - The reference: `nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, num_channels)` - Default: `affine=True`, `eps=1e-5` My kernel: ```cpp float mean = ...; mean /= channels_per_group; float var = ...; var /= channels_per_group; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); acc[oc] = (acc[oc] - mean) * inv_std * gamma[oc] + beta[oc]; ``` Wait, GroupNorm computes variance across ALL elements in the group. For group g with 8 channels and spatial size H*W = 258*258: - Total elements = 8 * 258 * 258 But in my kernel, I compute mean across the 8 channels at a single spatial position: ```cpp for (int g = 0; g < num_groups; ++g) { float mean = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < channels_per_group; ++c) { mean += acc[g * channels_per_group + c]; } mean /= channels_per_group; // Mean across 8 channels at one position ``` This is WRONG! GroupNorm computes mean and variance across ALL elements in the group (across both channels AND spatial positions). My kernel computes per-position normalization across channels, which is more like LayerNorm across a subset of channels at each position. GroupNorm should normalize across ALL positions in the group. Compute GroupNorm properly across all positions (difficult in a fused kernel without storing intermediates) 2. Use a simpler approach: PyTorch ConvTranspose2d, then a separate GELU+GroupNorm kernel Given the complexity, let me go with option 2: use PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d and write a correct GELU+GroupNorm kernel. For the GELU+GroupNorm kernel: - Grid: (N, num_groups) - Block: 256 threads - For each (n, g): - Load input elements and apply GELU - Compute sum and sum of squares across all elements in the group - Compute mean and inv_std - Apply normalization to all elements The challenge is that we have Cg * H * W elements per group (8 * 258 * 258 ≈ 532K elements), and we need to reduce over all of them. Each block has 256 threads. Approach: 1. Each thread processes a chunk of elements, computing local sum and sum of squares 2. Block-level reduction using warp shuffles and shared memory 3. Then each thread re-reads its elements and applies the normalization Since we need to read elements twice (once for stats, once for normalization), and storing all elements would require too much shared memory, we accept the re-computation. Let me write this kernel. Actually, wait. I can optimize by having each thread work on a contiguous chunk. The output is in NCHW format (from PyTorch ConvTranspose2d). So for sample n and group g starting at channel c0: - Elements are at: input[n, c, h, w] for c in [c0, c0+Cg), h in [0, H), w in [0, W) - In memory: contiguous in W dimension, then H, then C. - Total elements = Cg * H * W. Layout in memory (NCHW): offset = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w For contiguous access, we should iterate in the innermost dimension (W) first. With 256 threads and ~532K elements, each thread handles ~2082 elements. Let me write the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void gelu_groupnorm_kernel( const float* input, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups ) { int n = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int Cg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * Cg; int total_elements = Cg * H * W; float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sumsq = 0.0f; // First pass: compute GELU and accumulate statistics for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int rem = idx; int c = rem / (H * W); rem %= (H * W); int h = rem / W; int w = rem % W; int ic = c0 + c; float v = input[((n * C + ic) * H + h) * W + w]; float gv = gelu_exact(v); local_sum += gv; local_sumsq += gv * gv; } // Block reduction local_sum = block_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sumsq = block_reduce_sum(local_sumsq); __shared__ float s_mean, s_inv_std; if (tid == 0) { float mean = local_sum / total_elements; float var = local_sumsq / total_elements - mean * mean; s_mean = mean; s_inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } __syncthreads(); // Second pass: apply normalization for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int rem = idx; int c = rem / (H * W); rem %= (H * W); int h = rem / W; int w = rem % W; int ic = c0 + c; float v = input[((n * C + ic) * H + h) * W + w]; float gv = gelu_exact(v); float out_val = (gv - s_mean) * s_inv_std * gamma[ic] + beta[ic]; output[((n * C + ic) * H + h) * W + w] = out_val; } } ``` Wait, the issue is that `local_sum` and `local_sumsq` are both reduced, but I need to store the reduced result and broadcast it. Let me think about the block_reduce_sum function: ```cpp __device__ inline float block_reduce_sum(float val) { static __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < 32) ? shared[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warp_reduce_sum(val); return val; } ``` This stores the per-warp sum in shared memory, then one warp reduces those sums. But after this, only thread 0 has the correct total sum. Wait, no - warp_reduce_sum broadcasts the result to all threads in the warp. And then when warp 0 reads all values from shared memory and reduces, the result is broadcast to all threads in warp 0. But threads in other warps don't get the final result! Actually, let me trace through: 1. Each thread computes `val = warp_reduce_sum(local_val)` - now all threads in each warp have the warp's sum 2. Thread 0 of each warp writes to shared[wid] 3. __syncthreads() 4. All threads with tid < 32 read: `val = (tid < 32) ? shared[tid] : 0` - But shared was only written by threads with lane == 0. So threads 0, 32, 64, ... each wrote to shared[0, 1, 2, ...]. - Thread 0 reads shared[0], thread 1 reads shared[1], etc. All threads < 32 read a valid value. 5. Threads with wid == 0 (first warp) do `val = warp_reduce_sum(val)` - now threads in first warp have the total sum. 6. But threads in other warps still have their old `val` from step 1! So after block_reduce_sum, only threads in warp 0 have the correct total. This is wrong! I need to broadcast the result to all threads. A correct block_reduce_sum: ```cpp __device__ inline float block_reduce_sum(float val) { __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); if (wid == 0) { val = (lane < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[0] = val; } __syncthreads(); return shared[0]; } ``` This ensures only warp 0 does the final reduction and stores the result to shared[0]. Then all threads read shared[0] after __syncthreads(). The result is broadcast to everyone. OK, let me write the complete correct kernel now. Actually, I realize another simplification: I can use `torch.nn.functional.gelu` in PyTorch which is already optimized, and just write a GroupNorm kernel that operates in-place after GELU. But applying GELU inline in the kernel avoids one memory round-trip. Let me write the complete fused GELU+GN kernel. And I'll use PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d. For my final submission, I'll do: 1. PyTorch ConvTranspose2d 2. Custom kernel for exact-GELU + GroupNorm This avoids having to implement ConvTranspose2d manually (which is complex and I had bugs with) while still getting a significant speedup from fusing GELU+GN. Wait, but if I use PyTorch's GELU first and then a custom GroupNorm kernel, that's only marginally better than the reference. The real win is fusing GELU+GN so we compute GELU on the fly during the stats computation. Let me write the complete code now and submit. Actually, I'm worried about compile and correctness times.
